  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_b2cccb63-c49c-413d-a10f-c18816d58aaf'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15307876824864733481">For twenty-five years, a reclusive American novelist has been writing at the desk she inherited from a young Chilean poet who disappeared at the hands of Pinochet's secret police, one day a girl claiming to be the poet's daughter arrives to take it away, sending the writer's life reeling. Across the ocean, in the leafy suburbs of London, a man caring for his dying wife dis</span>
  <span id="freeText15307876824864733481" style="display:none">For twenty-five years, a reclusive American novelist has been writing at the desk she inherited from a young Chilean poet who disappeared at the hands of Pinochet's secret police, one day a girl claiming to be the poet's daughter arrives to take it away, sending the writer's life reeling. Across the ocean, in the leafy suburbs of London, a man caring for his dying wife discovers, among her papers, a lock of hair that unravels a terrible secret. In Jerusalem, an antiques dealer slowly reassembles his father's study, plundered by the Nazis in Budapest in 1944.<br /><br />Connecting these stories is a desk of many drawers that exerts a power over those who possess it or have given it away. As the narrators of Great House make their confessions, the desk takes on more and more meaning, and comes finally to stand for all that has been taken from them, and all that binds them to what has disappeared.<br /><br />Great House is a story haunted by questions: What do we pass on to our children and how do they absorb our dreams and losses? How do we respond to disappearance, destruction, and change?<br /><br />Nicole Krauss has written a soaring, powerful novel about memory struggling to creat a meaningful permanence in the face of inevitable loss.<br /><br />(front flap)</span>
  <a data-text-id="15307876824864733481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/708148-i-m-looking-for-the-connection-if-any">I&#39;m looking for the connection if any between Nadia, the judge, and the desk. Is there any? Other than that nadia was in Israel looking to find Leah and the desk when she hit the judge while driving? And was there a connection between Weisz and lotte berg even though Weisz seemed to deny it? And who was daniel varnsky? Was he lotte&#39;s son? or did she give him the desk because he reminded her of her son?</a>

<span class='communityAnswererName'>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">as far as i understood it,<br/>- nadia and the judge (dov) are connected only by the accident - and the judge isn't connected to anyone else in the story.<br/>-<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">as far as i understood it,<br />- nadia and the judge (dov) are connected only by the accident - and the judge isn't connected to anyone else in the story.<br />- there was no connection between lotte and weisz, he was only the person hired to find the desk.<br />- daniel varsky wasn't lotte's son - the son died, as we see in the last part of the book. he only reminded her of her son.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=stories-no-wait-a-novel-no-wait">stories-no-wait-a-novel-no-wait</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=vote-getters">vote-getters</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer128415449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3939582158759981849">So I say again: writing a book of short stories, fitting them together Tetris-like, and calling it a novel DOES NOT MAKE YOUR BOOK A NOVEL. Also telling your publisher to put "a novel" on the cover after the title DOES NOT MAKE YOUR BOOK A NOVEL. If you write a collection of short stories, IT IS OK TO CALL IT A COLLECTION OF SHORT STORIES. Because you are Nicole Krauss, especially, because you will probably STILL BE NOMINATED FOR THE NATIONAL BOOK AWARD. <br/><br/>***<br/><br/>(EDIT: But YOU WON'T WIN, thankfully! </span>
  <span id="freeText3939582158759981849" style="display:none">So I say again: writing a book of short stories, fitting them together Tetris-like, and calling it a novel DOES NOT MAKE YOUR BOOK A NOVEL. Also telling your publisher to put "a novel" on the cover after the title DOES NOT MAKE YOUR BOOK A NOVEL. If you write a collection of short stories, IT IS OK TO CALL IT A COLLECTION OF SHORT STORIES. Because you are Nicole Krauss, especially, because you will probably STILL BE NOMINATED FOR THE NATIONAL BOOK AWARD. <br /><br />***<br /><br />(EDIT: But YOU WON'T WIN, thankfully! <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9505561.Lord_of_Misrule" title="Lord of Misrule by Jaimy Gordon" rel="nofollow noopener">Lord of Misrule</a> will win.)<br /><br />***<br /><br />This is a very well written book throughout, meaning sentence by sentence. Nicole Krauss can string words together effortlessly, can create haunting and memorable imagery through evocative metaphors you'd never dream of. But... all those sentences didn't add up to much of anything for me. It definitely feels like the form is what interests her, but the Big Idea (interconnected stories that are all related to the perhaps malovelent presence of an imposing desk, its many tiny drawers standing in for the melodramatic seekrits! and secret pains of its many owners) obscures the characters.<br /><br />It sounds interesting, but it isn't. It's monotonous, and confusing. Puzzling out the structure is a pain in the neck because the stories, each with a different narrator, all sound the same, whether the speaker is a middle-aged American woman or an elderly male Hungarian Jew. Everyone mopes about the WEIGHT OF MEMORY (this was probably explained to me on the jacket copy because I honestly had a hard enough time concentrating on the plot), which is symbolized by furniture and also by how they constantly talk about their horrible memories. I don't have to like the characters, but not a one of them had much of a spark, which made reading about them distasteful AND dull.<br /><br />I can't say I'm surprised this was nominated for the National Book Award, but I am disappointed, because I think if it wins, a lot of extra people are going to read it, and a lot of them aren't going to like it. And I read a lot of other books this year, books that weren't nominated, that I think would benefit from the extra attention and might also be a lot less likely to alienate readers. For all of the depth of talent on display in the prose, this is an oddly lifeless book, and I don't see it connecting with most readers, even "serious readers."<br /><br />In conclusion: I would not recommend Great House: A Collection of Stories and I certainly don't recommend Great House: A Novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3939582158759981849" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2098234004" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2098234004" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2098234004?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=jewish-books">jewish-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=holocaust">holocaust</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2098234004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4626240121399237552">As I sit down to assess the past year with Rosh Hashanah fast approaching, I decided to read a Jewish author who I have never read before. Recently in one of the groups I am in here on Goodreads- the Reading for Pleasure book group- I took a turn holding the quill for the group's Pepys Project, a diary detailing literary births, deaths, and happenings for each day. The last day of my turn was August 18, the birthday of author <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2633.Nicole_Krauss' title='Nicole Krauss' rel='nofollow noopener'>Nicole Krauss</a>. With her new book <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33544874.Forest_Dark' title='Forest Dark by Nicole Krauss' rel='nofollow noopener'>Forest Dark</a> due to hit shelves soon, </span>
  <span id="freeText4626240121399237552" style="display:none">As I sit down to assess the past year with Rosh Hashanah fast approaching, I decided to read a Jewish author who I have never read before. Recently in one of the groups I am in here on Goodreads- the Reading for Pleasure book group- I took a turn holding the quill for the group's Pepys Project, a diary detailing literary births, deaths, and happenings for each day. The last day of my turn was August 18, the birthday of author <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2633.Nicole_Krauss" title="Nicole Krauss" rel="nofollow noopener">Nicole Krauss</a>. With her new book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33544874.Forest_Dark" title="Forest Dark by Nicole Krauss" rel="nofollow noopener">Forest Dark</a> due to hit shelves soon, I felt that this was a way of telling me that I should experience the award winning author's works for myself. Reading descriptions carefully, I decided upon <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7907782.Great_House" title="Great House by Nicole Krauss" rel="nofollow noopener">Great House</a>, a title evoking imagery of the destruction of the Temple in Jerusalem with a multi-layered plot full of surprises along the way.<br /><br />From the novels first pages, it was evident to me that Krauss is both a leading Jewish novelist and literary fiction writer today. The book starts out where Nadia, a writer perhaps meant to be Krauss herself, is talking to a judge. In a stream of consciousness monologue, Nadia takes her readers down memory lane as she describes her life as a novelist over the last twenty five years. While the story takes place in the present, Nadia's entire existence is rooted with a chance meeting with Chilean poet named Daniel Varsky twenty six years before. Although their affair was brief, Varsky left Nadia his immense desk. This desk, which held more than just sentimental value to Varsky, becomes the focal point of Nadia's career, as she sits there to write seven novels, which become her livelihood. It is during work on the eighth novel that Varsky's supposed daughter comes to ask for the desk back, sending Nadia's life and career into a tailspin.<br /><br />In each section of the novel, Krauss introduces another protagonist whose life has been impacted by this grand desk. Originally the property of a prominent Jewish family in Hungary, the desk had been plundered by the Nazis and underwent a journey across four continents until it reached its present destination in Nadia's apartment. In a London suburb, Arthur Berg cares for his wife Lotte who is dying of Alzheimer's. Lotte had also been a writer and made the acquaintance of Varsky; she was so taken by him that she gifted him her desk, that had once been a gift from a previous lover. Berg did not realize the impact of either the desk or of Varsky on Lotte's life until her disease had overtaken most of her memory. Thus, the desk underwent more travels through history.<br /><br />Each protagonist is stronger than the other. Krauss introduces her readers to an Israeli attorney named Aaron who is reeling from the death of his wife Rivka. Aaron has to come to grips with his relationship with his adult son Dovik, who had always been introverted and closer with his mother. The family's relationship with the desk is not inherent from the start; however, Aaron's monologues describing Dovik's troubled childhood are among Krauss' most poignant passages in the novel. I can only help feelin for Dovik as he lived with the trauma growing up with a father who did not understand him and was much closer to his rival sibling.<br /><br />Finally, the plot comes together with the introduction of Weisz, a furniture dealer who has rescued countless pieces of antiques pilfered by the Nazis from places around the globe, and his children Yoav and Leah. Like the other characters in the novel, the triumvirate has a deeply personal relationship with the desk; however, as the family originally comes from eastern Europe, it becomes apparent that their history with this piece may be greater than that of the other characters. Even though each piece of plot moves slowly, Krauss' use of prose is exquisite and a joy to read. I could not help but reading quickly through this gem to find out the origins of the desk and who its rightful owner is. With emotions riding high as to the ownership, the second half of the novel moved faster than the first.<br /><br />Toward the end of the novel, Weisz delivers a poignant monologue explaining the novel's title. In the words of biblical scholar Rabbi Yochanan ben Zakkai, he explains that to "Bend a people around the shape of what they lost, and let everything mirror its absent form. Later his school became known as the Great House, after the phrase in Books of Kings: He burned the house of G-d, the king's house, and all houses of Jerusalem, even every great house he burned with fire." So close to the new year, I appreciate Krauss' use of biblical anecdotes to the Jewish people as people of the book. I thoroughly enjoyed this intricately woven novel of memories, a web of human emotions, and strong prose. I am sure that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7907782.Great_House" title="Great House by Nicole Krauss" rel="nofollow noopener">Great House</a> will not be the last of her novels that I read, as I rate this gem of a book 4.5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4626240121399237552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_150063271" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150063271" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150063271?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780670919338" href="/book/show/9454008-great-house">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=krauss">krauss</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2015">read-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150063271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14287740588321616533"><b>How Did She Do What She Just Did?</b><br/><br/>I looked forward to reading this novel for several years, was apprehensive in the first couple of chapters, persisted, got my bearings, then in the second half grew confident that it would blow my mind (which it did).<br/><br/>The novel makes demands on you, you have to exert yourself, but the rewards are enormous and profound.<br/><br/>As at the time of writing this review (if I can call it that), I finished the novel less than 24 hours ago. I still haven't worked out what else to </span>
  <span id="freeText14287740588321616533" style="display:none"><b>How Did She Do What She Just Did?</b><br /><br />I looked forward to reading this novel for several years, was apprehensive in the first couple of chapters, persisted, got my bearings, then in the second half grew confident that it would blow my mind (which it did).<br /><br />The novel makes demands on you, you have to exert yourself, but the rewards are enormous and profound.<br /><br />As at the time of writing this review (if I can call it that), I finished the novel less than 24 hours ago. I still haven't worked out what else to say about it. <br /><br />There is so much I want to talk about with somebody/anybody. However, because of the desire to avoid spoilers, I know there are things I mustn't say in a review. <br /><br />The problem is, it's these aspects of the novel that fascinate me, and they are so numerous, so I'm going to write whatever spoiler-free thing comes into my head relatively spontaneously. <br /><br />I'm sorry I can't do better than that yet, but I want to at least make a start, while the book is still fresh in my mind.<br /><br />One thing I must say, though, is that I admire what Nicole Krauss achieved as a writer in this novel. <br /><br />My immediate reaction was: how did she do what she just did? <br /><br />Somehow, she seemed to drag me along from the relatively mundane to the sublime. Only, I just never anticipated how sublime it would be.<br /><br /><b>The (Absence or Loss of) Plot</b><br /><br />It isn't a narrative-driven novel. There is no plot to speak of (or is there?). <br /><br />There is a sense in which Nicole Krauss is rebelling against the traditional plot. To that extent, this is an exemplary Post-Modernist work.<br /><br />The narratives are set at different times. Lots of dates are mentioned. The first thing I tried to do after I finished was to write down the dates and what happened. <br /><br />Some sort of chronology emerged. I moved the pieces around like a jigsaw puzzle, and bit by bit a clearer picture eventuated. But it's one you could keep returning to, year after year, trying to get better at reading or assembling it. <br /><br /><b>The Structure</b><br /><br />The book is less than 300 pages long. However, it took me almost a hundred pages to detect its rhythm and feel comfortable. I think this was because I had anticipated something quite different (possibly as a result of the blurb).<br /><br />The structure of the novel is quite simple to describe. However, aspects of it resemble and raise similar issues with respect to the juxtaposition of different narratives as a recent novel that consisted of nested stories:<br /><br />* It's divided into two parts.<br /><br />* The second part is almost a mirror image that expands on or resolves the first part.<br /><br />* Each part has four chapters (roughly 40 pages each).<br /><br />* Each chapter has a title. Three of the chapter titles are repeated in the second part. The fourth (and last chapter in the book) is given a new title, but relates to the same person/people. <br /><br />* The sequence of the chapters changes between the two parts of the novel.<br /><br />* Each chapter is narrated in the first person, more or less as a monologue. <br /><br />* There is relatively little dialogue. We really get into the head of each narrator, whether or not we like or empathise with them. <br /><br />* There are a few stylistic anomalies in some chapters (e.g., clumsy similes), which made me wonder whether Krauss' attention to detail might have slipped. However, ultimately, I decided that she knew what she was doing all along, and that this inelegance was a trait of the narrator, not the author.<br /><br /><b>The Journey</b><br /><br />The novel doesn't just progress from A to B, or from A to Z. However, regardless, the novel maps a journey. All of the steps are carefully recorded. <br /><br />By the time we get to the end of the novel, we're conscious of the journey as a whole. What seems to be fragmentary as we progress, is ultimately assembled together in a manner that accomplishes completeness, a whole, an entirety, a world, a universe, a Great House.<br /><br />Equally importantly, the journey is not just the journey of these narrators. Krauss has a unique ability to make the journey (seem like) our journey as well. <br /><br />I don't just mean this in the sense of empathy or verisimilitude. <br /><br />I mean that she makes us make it our journey as well. We have to exert ourselves. We personalise it. We don't just observe characters acting. It's almost as if we get up on stage and join them. We are trying as hard as them to make sense of this world that is being presented to all of us in fragments. <br /><br />This world that Krauss is portraying is not just their world. It's our world as well. We learn about ourselves as we learn about her narrators.<br /><br />I'm reluctant to describe the individual narrators or the chronology of events. <br /><br />It might seem trite to say that it's essential for you to experience them yourself as part of your journey through the world of the novel. I can't and don't want to take (and don't want to spoil) your journey for you. It has to be your own journey.<br /><br /><b>The Great House</b><br /><br />There are many times on the journey when you ask yourself what the Great House is.<br /><br />It could be many types of house, both literally and metaphorically: an actual physical home (with all of its furniture and contents, including a writing desk), a family (as in the House of Usher), a Temple, even a Book (whether holy or not).<br /><br />Whatever type of house, I think it's a defence or buttress against the abyss.<br /><br />Whenever I read the word <i>"abyss"</i> (particularly in the context of philosophy), I wonder how the word originated. <br /><br />I tend to visualise it as a hole or an emptiness. However, there's also a sense in which it is the opposite of being <i>"grounded"</i>: it might mean that we are <i>"un-grounded".</i> We have no solid, physical foundation upon which to stand or build a home. It might seem paradoxical, but just as we might fall into the abyss, we might float or fly away from solid ground, if we are un-grounded.<br /><br /><i>"Great House"</i> is concerned with this abyss and what it takes to be grounded, although not necessarily in so many words. <br /><br />The two words that come to mind for me are <i>"absence"</i> and <i>"loss".</i> In a way, both describe the non-presence of some object or characteristic or person. <br /><br /><i>"Absence"</i> could mean that it has never been present; <i>"loss"</i> might mean that it was once present, but is no longer so.<br /><br />The novel raises the question how do we (or should we) deal with the abyss, with the absence, with the loss.<br /><br />Each narrator is missing something, whether or not it has always been absent, or whether it has been lost, or whether it has been burned or stolen, or whether it has been given away (whether permanently or temporarily).<br /><br />Each narrator is estranged by the absence or loss. Each narrator tries to do something about it in their own unique way.<br /><br />It's questionable whether you can overcome the absence or loss by yourself. Although the absence or loss might apply to a physical object (like the desk), it applies equally to relationships (like love).<br /><br />Krauss explores these issues in the context of the Jewish predicament. However, I think it is equally applicable to other religions and cultures.<br /><br />The Jewish people have had to deal with two challenges: the loss of Jerusalem, and the Holocaust.<br /><br />One response to the loss of Jerusalem is to:<br /><br /><i>"Turn Jerusalem into an idea. Turn the Temple into a book, a book as vast and holy and intricate as the city itself. Bend a people around the shape of what they lost, and let everything mirror its absent form...each one of us [can] only recall the tiniest fragment: a pattern on the wall, a knot in the wood of a door, a memory of how light fell across the floor. <br /><br />"But if every Jewish memory were put together, every last holy fragment joined up again as one, the House would be built again...or rather a memory of the House so perfect that it would be, in essence, the original itself...a perfect assemblage of the infinite parts of the Jewish memory...we live, each of us, to preserve our fragment, in a state of perpetual regret and longing for a place we only know existed because we remember a key hole, a tile, the way the threshold was worn under an open door."</i><br /><br />So memory is part of the mechanism by which we combat the abyss, and books are the depositories of memories.<br /><br />Many of the characters react to the absence or loss by way of their silence. They internalise and bottle up their anguish.<br /><br />Sometimes, you need to be opened up or uncorked or unlocked. Sometimes, you are the key hole, and somebody else holds the key. Sometimes, you have to realise that you yourself hold the key. <br /><br />Together, our fragments form a perfect assemblage. Together, we form a Great House.<br /><br />This novel is a Great House assembled out of the fragments of real or imaginary lives. By the time the novel is finished, its shape becomes apparent, and everything locks into place. It is a truly holy book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14287740588321616533" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4511412114923081486">I’m surprised this was written after History of Love because for me, though perhaps more grown up, it’s less accomplished. The design is brilliant but let down by the execution. There are four first person narratives, all of them Jewish. The Holocaust is rarely overtly mentioned but it haunts the entire novel. Its memorial is a desk that connects all these people. One problem I had was all these voices tend to ramble, all go off point. There are entire paragraphs which could be removed without i</span>
  <span id="freeText4511412114923081486" style="display:none">I’m surprised this was written after History of Love because for me, though perhaps more grown up, it’s less accomplished. The design is brilliant but let down by the execution. There are four first person narratives, all of them Jewish. The Holocaust is rarely overtly mentioned but it haunts the entire novel. Its memorial is a desk that connects all these people. One problem I had was all these voices tend to ramble, all go off point. There are entire paragraphs which could be removed without in any way altering the book’s design. Often there was a brilliant page followed by a page of hot air. And these characters aren’t as memorable as those in History of Love. They’re all also rather unlikeable. Control freaks. No doubt this was intended as a depiction of the repercussions of carrying Nazis atrocities in your genes but I felt it could have been achieved with more elasticity, more flair, more, dare I say it, charm. History of Love almost had a surfeit of charm after all. <br /><br />It’s also a detective novel and I greatly enjoyed figuring out all the connections. I admired it but I didn’t quite love it.</span>

<div id="review_2044082170" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2044082170" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2044082170?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2044082170" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14442826067785404743">After reading The History of Love, I promised myself to read something else by Nicole Krauss when I had the chance. I found Great House at a local thrift store for $1, and it was one of the best dollars I ever spent. <br/><br/>There are several narratives to follow and they are tied together by a desk, a desk that was part of the stolen property of Jews displaced by the Third Reich. Each of the narratives is a story in itself, a glimpse into the lives of people who struggle with their humanity and how the</span>
  <span id="freeText14442826067785404743" style="display:none">After reading The History of Love, I promised myself to read something else by Nicole Krauss when I had the chance. I found Great House at a local thrift store for $1, and it was one of the best dollars I ever spent. <br /><br />There are several narratives to follow and they are tied together by a desk, a desk that was part of the stolen property of Jews displaced by the Third Reich. Each of the narratives is a story in itself, a glimpse into the lives of people who struggle with their humanity and how they fit into the world at large. What each story has at its core is the theme of loss, memory and isolation. Many of these characters have an excruciating inability to reach beyond themselves and touch others or let anyone in.<br /><br />This book is written like a maze, weaving in and out, characters coming and going. It is a puzzle with pieces that lie just out of the reach of your hand and without which you can never make a complete picture. There were moments when I wondered if I had missed something crucial, I felt so lost, and then Krauss would lay down her next layer and I would find the pieces interlocking and making sense. There was, within that revelation, an accompanying feeling that I had discovered something not only about the characters, but about myself.<br /><br />This is a book that raises important questions and leaves you pondering answers long after you have reached the final page. How much can you know about another person? Does a person deserve to have his secrets respected after death? How long can you close a person out before it is too late to make amends? Can we ever understand a person fully if we do not have access to their history, their stories, their losses? How can we not live with death every day, when we all know death is the ultimate outcome for each of us?<br /><br />And then there is the concept of a thing carrying the memory, and in some way the residual life, of those who are lost. I know from experience that when a person is gone, any possession they cherished takes on a different meaning. In some ways, it can come to embody the idea of that person and feel like a bridge to their soul. And, we can be linked in our minds to our pasts by smells and textures and breezes that blow through windows carrying sea spray or the smell of roses. We can sometimes feel that if we could recreate those things, that material world, we could repossess our lost lives, our childhood, or our loves.<br /><br />Krauss writes prose that flows and sings and carries you along like a river. For example, she describes an Alzheimer’s patient in words that capture perfectly what those of us who have known the progress of this disease easily recognize.<br /> <br /><i>I could see in her eyes that beneath those words there was nothing, just an abyss, like the black-water pond she disappeared into every morning no matter the weather. Then followed a period when she became scared, aware of how much she was losing by the day, perhaps even the hour, like a person slowly bleeding to death, hemorrhaging toward oblivion...And then even that period passed, and she no longer remembered enough to be afraid, no longer remembered, I suppose, that things had ever been any other way, and from then on she set off along, utterly alone, on a long journey back to the shores of her childhood.</i><br /><br />If I was jolted by her transition from story to story, I was pulled back into the story immediately by her use of description and language. The thread may have been tenuous at times, but it was worth any effort required to follow the thread to its end.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14442826067785404743" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_128369714" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/128369714" />
    <a title="Kemper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/405390-kemper"><img alt="Kemper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495476851p2/405390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/128369714?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kemper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kemper" href="/user/show/405390-kemper">Kemper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=plain-old-fiction">plain-old-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=2011">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer128369714" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7674766252789931902">I’m more a genre guy than a literature reader, but I’ve been trying to branch out lately. I’m glad I did because I’ve read some amazing things that I probably wouldn’t have tried otherwise. However, it only takes one book like this send me running back to the mystery or sci-fi section for comfort. It wasn’t bad, but it’s just working so damn hard to be an ‘important’ book that it really isn’t much fun to read. And maybe all books shouldn’t be fun, but they really shouldn’t feel like this much wo</span>
  <span id="freeText7674766252789931902" style="display:none">I’m more a genre guy than a literature reader, but I’ve been trying to branch out lately. I’m glad I did because I’ve read some amazing things that I probably wouldn’t have tried otherwise. However, it only takes one book like this send me running back to the mystery or sci-fi section for comfort. It wasn’t bad, but it’s just working so damn hard to be an ‘important’ book that it really isn’t much fun to read. And maybe all books shouldn’t be fun, but they really shouldn’t feel like this much work either.<br /><br />The book begins in the early 1970s in New York with a writer named Nadia losing all her furniture due to a break up with a boyfriend. A mutual friend steers Nadia to Daniel Varsky, a young Chilean poet who is getting ready to leave New York and has an apartment full of furniture he wants to loan out until he returns. The most impressive item is a large desk. Nadia takes the furniture and later hears that Daniel was tortured and killed in Chile during Pinochet’s brutal rule of the country.<br /><br />Years pass and the one constant in Nadia’s life is the desk. However, when a young woman claiming to be Daniel’s daughter from a fling he had in Israel shows up, Nadia immediately relinquishes the desk to her, but soon regrets it.<br /><br />Several other stories are told in parallel to Nadia’s. An Israeli man mourning the death of his wife pours his heart out in a story to the son he never understood. The husband of a British writer discovers a shocking secret about his wife after her death, and a young woman reflects on her love for a man who had an odd relationship with his sister and their father who is trying to recreate the study of his childhood home that was lost in the Holocaust. Eventually, the links between all of the stories emerge.<br /><br />Krauss is one of those writers who impresses me technically but leaves me a bit cold despite writing something that was obviously going for the heart. A big part of my problem is that that four of the characters are almost exactly the same. Nadia, the British writer, the young woman in love, and the Israeli son are introverted types who live their lives mainly through books and words to the point of ignoring everything else. I especially found Nadia tiresome because this is a woman with every advantage who deliberately chooses her writing career over relationships yet whines about her own nature constantly. It’s hard to feel too sympathetic for someone who cut themselves off of their own free will and yet who is so fragile that the loss of a desk will plunge them into a depressive bout of writer’s block.<br /><br />The plot comes together in a nice web of cause-and-effect, but overall this book felt like getting stuck in a conversation with someone who obviously wants to be doing something else, but then proceeds to tell you about everything they’ve talked about with their psychiatrist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7674766252789931902" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1661732832" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1661732832" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1661732832?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=jewish">jewish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=american">american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1661732832" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2313578290333775250"><b>How to Extract Empathy</b><br/><br/>Krauss is a mistress of extracted empathy. She can drag it out of you even when you fight it, particularly empathy for writers: for Nadia, a writer prevented by success from writing what she ought; for Dov, an Israeli, prevented by apparent paternal sadism from becoming a writer at all; for Lotte, an Holocaust-traumatised emigre writer, who reportedly goes skinny dipping every day on Hampstead Heath; for Isabel, a failed Oxford student (presumably a writer, if only of essay</span>
  <span id="freeText2313578290333775250" style="display:none"><b>How to Extract Empathy</b><br /><br />Krauss is a mistress of extracted empathy. She can drag it out of you even when you fight it, particularly empathy for writers: for Nadia, a writer prevented by success from writing what she ought; for Dov, an Israeli, prevented by apparent paternal sadism from becoming a writer at all; for Lotte, an Holocaust-traumatised emigre writer, who reportedly goes skinny dipping every day on Hampstead Heath; for Isabel, a failed Oxford student (presumably a writer, if only of essays), who makes some bizarre personal decisions. Their stories touch each other just enough to amplify the empathy one feels for each.<br /><br />It intrigues me how she does this. The fundamental theme is one of alienation - from loved ones, from family, from the world, from oneself - as described by four narrators. But the variations on this theme overlay each other to absorb the reader into the desperation of each. How are they connected? Are the stories about the teller or the one told about? Is the central theme the awkwardness of living with authors, even with oneself as an author? Or is the real story that of people coping with emotions buried so deeply that they can only be alluded to and discovered in a sort of psychoanalytic process carried out in print?<br /><br />The literary devices in Great House are as complex as the variations on the basic theme. The thin thread of a piece of furniture is used to keep the parts together. But there are numerous recurring tropes: The collapse and inversion of time over decades; the random interjection of discontinuous events; the phrase “after what happened” without explanation, used unexpectedly to queue up a later revelation; narrative characters (and narrators) left unidentified for extended periods; the teasingly repeated denial of expected resolutions; open family secrets never discussed; the homecoming after years of absence; and withal the withholding of any suggestion of purpose until the end. The effect is one of not just suspense but an experience of a pressing need to know how the characters survive, if indeed they do.<br /><br />So, a complex, challenging and rewarding work by a pro. Can’t ask for much more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2313578290333775250" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_125801665" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125801665" />
    <a title="Ursula" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1677559-ursula"><img alt="Ursula" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1359339341p2/1677559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125801665?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ursula" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ursula" href="/user/show/1677559-ursula">Ursula</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1677559-ursula?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125801665" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10411705833917065412">This is the worst book I've read in years! The narratives are incredibly disjointed and confusing. None of the characters is interesting enough to warrant the energy required of the reader to piece together their stories in a meaningful way. The writing itself is trite and one gets the feeling that one has read similar stories by better writers. By far the worst flaw of the book is the lack of propulsion. I'm amazed that I read the entire book as there was nothing driving the book forward. Witho</span>
  <span id="freeText10411705833917065412" style="display:none">This is the worst book I've read in years! The narratives are incredibly disjointed and confusing. None of the characters is interesting enough to warrant the energy required of the reader to piece together their stories in a meaningful way. The writing itself is trite and one gets the feeling that one has read similar stories by better writers. By far the worst flaw of the book is the lack of propulsion. I'm amazed that I read the entire book as there was nothing driving the book forward. Without giving too much of the novel away, I will say the image of the shark was arresting and beautiful but that the furniture motif felt forced and fell flat. Why this book was nominated for the National Book Award is beyond me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10411705833917065412" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1110201292" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1110201292" />
    <a title="Violet wells" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells"><img alt="Violet wells" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442260743p2/4196681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1110201292?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Violet wells" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Violet wells" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells">Violet wells</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=21st-century">21st-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=contemporary-american-fiction">contemporary-american-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=faves">faves</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1110201292" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer701041477518041715">A common criticism of this book is that it’s more like four short stories than a novel. It’s true the four narratives, with a little tinkering, could stand alone as brilliant inspired stories. There’s a suspicion too that Nicole Krauss has difficulties writing novels. Only two in ten years – in stark contrast to someone like Murakami who knocks them out with what’s becoming almost a facile (and self-harming) ease. The stories are connected by a mysterious writing desk (reminiscent of Edmund de W</span>
  <span id="freeText701041477518041715" style="display:none">A common criticism of this book is that it’s more like four short stories than a novel. It’s true the four narratives, with a little tinkering, could stand alone as brilliant inspired stories. There’s a suspicion too that Nicole Krauss has difficulties writing novels. Only two in ten years – in stark contrast to someone like Murakami who knocks them out with what’s becoming almost a facile (and self-harming) ease. The stories are connected by a mysterious writing desk (reminiscent of Edmund de Waal’s The Hare with Amber Eyes when it’s a collection of Japanese netsuke that are used to follow bloodlines). Though the desk vanishes for great stretches of the narrative it haunts the lives of every character in these pages. Initially the desk is another victim of the Nazis. It vanishes from the home of a Jewish family in Germany. At the heart of the book is an antiques dealer who restores furniture looted by the Nazis to their Jewish owners. So the desk becomes a symbol of both home and heritage and in Great House we see how it affects the lives of a truly fabulous cast of characters. The characters are so vividly engaging that we miss them when they are gone – which is probably my only misgiving about this book. It’s difficult to keep the bigger picture in mind because of the overwhelming sweep and luminosity of each new page. It’s a bit like falling in love when the new lover utterly eclipses all who have come before. But it has to be said that there’s more brilliant writing in this book than any I’ve read this year so, on the whole, a massive thumbs up from me. And just wish Nicole Krauss was more prolific. </span>
  <a data-text-id="701041477518041715" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_124594753" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124594753" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/520753-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402541317p2/520753.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/124594753?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/520753-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/520753-kim?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/520753-kim?shelf=mmxi">mmxi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/520753-kim?shelf=cultured">cultured</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer124594753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6444665097282560050">This book is not about a house, great or minute. It’s about a<a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[desk. <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br/><br/>Okay, so maybe metaphorically speaking it could be about a great house, like as if we all live in the ‘great house’ of life blah blah blah but, really, it’s about a<a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[desk. <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br/><br/>I’m not complaining. I really really like the <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[desk. <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> (aside: you can actually click on those... It's not a real spoiler, I just wanted to test that feature out.) It sounds</span>
  <span id="freeText6444665097282560050" style="display:none">This book is not about a house, great or minute. It’s about a<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[desk. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />Okay, so maybe metaphorically speaking it could be about a great house, like as if we all live in the ‘great house’ of life blah blah blah but, really, it’s about a<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[desk. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />I’m not complaining. I really really like the <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[desk. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> (aside: you can actually click on those... It's not a real spoiler, I just wanted to test that feature out.) It sounds like it could hold a house with all its drawers and it’s magnitude and daunting history. <i>“To call it a desk is to say too little. The word conjures some homely, unassuming article of work or domesticity, a selfless and practical object that is always poised to offer up its back for its owner too make use of, and which, when not in use, occupies its allotted space with humility….This desk was something else entirely; an enormous, forboding thing that bore down on the occupants of the room it inhabited, pretending to be inanimate but, like a Venus flytrap, ready to pounce on them and digest them via one of its many little terrible drawers.”</i><br /> <br />I am sure that I am not worthy of this desk. Desks like these are for <b>S</b>ignificant <b>P</b>eople. I am comfortable in knowing I am not one of them. Except to my six year old, who has yet to learn. <br /><br />I wasn’t sure how I was going to feel at the end of this book. It’s sort of… aloof. It has a presence and it’s not one to embrace you and absorb you within its pages being so unkind as to make you ignore your family, friends, traffic lights. No, it’s dignified. It stands there and says ‘OK, I’m here and I will tell you a story but you need to follow along, I’m not going to hold your hand, I’m not going to wait for you—don’t try to hug me. Personal space, buddy.’ It’s been awhile since I’ve read something like this and at first the psychoneurotic in me pushed it away. I didn’t feel the demand and it made me stand offish. <br /><br />I’m not sure where the switch happened. Where I couldn’t put the book down even though it still had me at arms length. Was it at <i>"I tapped a giant bruise on my knee that I couldn’t remember getting. I’ve reached the age where bruises are formed from failures within rather than accidents without.”</i> or was it <i>“… silence was not so much a form of evasion as a way for solitary people to coexist in a family.”</i> I think I just sort of erected a shanty around it. Each section is really its own story and the one thread is the desk. Krauss takes her time connecting them, instead introducing you to new characters or revisiting characters from early on. It can be infuriating if you’re looking for flow but I’m not, so all’s well. I have not read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3867.The_History_of_Love" title="The History of Love by Nicole Krauss" rel="nofollow noopener">The History of Love</a> yet; I’ve started it and it kind of bored me so…. but I’m sure it was my fault, not the books (it’s never the book’s fault.) so I wasn’t familiar with her writing style. I have to say that it made me feel brainy. You know how some books make you sad or mad or bored or angry? This made me feel mature. Each section reads (to me) like a telling. The character is in a confessional and what you read is raw, exposing, looking for absolution. It made me guffaw to come across her quoting Camus ‘The act of love is always a confession.’ See? Me=Smart. <br /><br />But, what can I really say about this book? I liked it. You may not. I tend to like female writers, not really sure why, but I do. I enjoyed watching the history of the desk, of what it meant to each character or how it affected others. There were lines that made me sigh: <i>"There are times when the kindness of strangers only makes matters worse because one realizes how badly one is in need of kindness and that the only source is a stranger.” </i><br />This will stick with me. I will compare near future books with this one. It won’t wear off so soon. <br /><br />There were two quotes where different characters refer to books that I read more than once then jotted down in my notebook:<br /><br /><i>“The idea of being weighed down made me uneasy, as if I lived on the surface of a frozen lake and each new trapping of domestic life—a pot, a chair, a lamp--- threatened to be the thing that sent me through the ice. The only exception was books, which I acquired freely, because I never really felt they belonged to me. Because of this, I never felt compelled to finish those I didn’t like, or even a pressure to like them at all. But a certain lack of responsibility also left me free to be affected. When at last I came across the right book the feeling was violent: it blew open a hole in me that made life more dangerous because I couldn’t control what came through it.”</i><br /><br />You’re preaching to the choir, Nicole.<br /><br /><i>“I spent the morning reading Ovid. I read differently know, more painstakingly, knowing I am probably revisiting the books I love for the last time.”</i><br /><br />What a sad thought, especially when I can’t remember a lot of the books that I love, but I guess that’s something I have to let go of, for now. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6444665097282560050" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1634002656" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1634002656" />
    <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate"><img alt="Roger Brunyate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462877641p2/42294189.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1634002656?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roger Brunyate" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate">Roger Brunyate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=holocaust">holocaust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=israel">israel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1634002656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1189309179568326280"><b>If I Forget Thee…</b><br/><br/>Let me say it up front: Nicole Krauss is a major writer at the height of her powers and her latest novel is a towering achievement. Her subject is loss, and a process of reconstruction that is always painful and inevitably only partial. Loss, of course, is a central theme for many Jewish writers of her generation, but Krauss has dealt with it with greater consistency than most. Her first novel, <i><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/44380.Man_Walks_Into_a_Room' title='Man Walks Into a Room by Nicole Krauss' rel='nofollow noopener'>Man Walks Into a Room</a>,</i> treated the subject obliquely, through a protagonist who loses</span>
  <span id="freeText1189309179568326280" style="display:none"><b>If I Forget Thee…</b><br /><br />Let me say it up front: Nicole Krauss is a major writer at the height of her powers and her latest novel is a towering achievement. Her subject is loss, and a process of reconstruction that is always painful and inevitably only partial. Loss, of course, is a central theme for many Jewish writers of her generation, but Krauss has dealt with it with greater consistency than most. Her first novel, <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/44380.Man_Walks_Into_a_Room" title="Man Walks Into a Room by Nicole Krauss" rel="nofollow noopener">Man Walks Into a Room</a>,</i> treated the subject obliquely, through a protagonist who loses all his adult memories as the result of brain tumor and must find ways of constructing a new life in his spiritual exile. Although her second, <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3867.The_History_of_Love" title="The History of Love by Nicole Krauss" rel="nofollow noopener">The History of Love</a>,</i> has something of the quality of fable, it tackles the subject more directly, by bringing together the stories of a Jewish boy writing in Poland before the Holocaust and a teenage girl in New York in the present day. In it, Krauss introduced the idea of using two or more separate stories that come together only at the end, not necessarily in the ways one might expect; here, she takes the approach a great deal farther. For fragmentation is a tragic reality of the Jewish experience, and with this novel Nicole Krauss makes diaspora into a literary technique.<br /><br />With <i>Great House</i>, Krauss leaves behind the almost childlike quality of her previous novel and takes possession of her maturity like a mansion. The four voices whose monologues make up most of the book all belong to people of middle age or older; they are people whose business is words and ideas; they have lived lives complex enough to include both achievement and regret; they describe themselves with a merciless clarity that does not, however, exclude the possibility of change. Their stories are perplexingly unconnected. A successful novelist in New York is visited by the daughter of a murdered Chilean poet whom she had known in her youth, and requests the return of a desk that he lent to her. An elderly Israeli lawyer, sitting shiva for his wife, is joined by his estranged son, now a distinguished British judge. At another funeral in London, an Oxford professor thinks back over his long marriage to his own late wife, and of those parts of her life that she kept resolutely private, even from him. An American scholar recalls the time she also spent shuttling between Oxford and London, and her friendship with the two children of a reclusive man who runs an international business in antiques based in Jerusalem.<br /><br />As we read, we inevitably look for connections between these stories, only to find that the few clues do not seem to link up. Instead we start to find thematic connections: roots and rootlessness; the almost arbitrary importance of possessions; parents dominating or neglecting their children; the use of writing to make sense of a shattered life; the loneliness of having to choose between the peopled world and the inner haven of ideas. Although the four speakers are distinct, each of the sections is richly textured, challenging the reader to keep a tight grasp on the increasing complexity of the structure as a whole; those tottering nested boxes on the front cover turn out to be a most relevant image. The one thing that does seem to connect most (but at first not all) of the stories is the poet's desk, and we begin to understand the symbolic importance of recovering objects that recall a life before old age, before the waning of inspiration, before torture and death, before the Holocaust. But we also learn the secret of another kind of identity that can survive the loss of property or the destruction of Solomon's Temple: the temple of ideas, of laws and knowledge, the Great House of thought and belief that can transcend diaspora.<br /><br />Important ideas seldom occur in isolation. The structure of almost disconnected narratives here reminded me a little of Frederick Reiken's brilliant debut novel <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7530820.Day_for_Night" title="Day for Night by Frederick Reiken" rel="nofollow noopener">Day for Night</a>,</i> but with a much longer attention span. Some sections of the Israeli jurist's memories of the failed upbringing of his son seemed uncannily close to David Grossman's recent <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7779571.To_the_End_of_the_Land" title="To the End of the Land by David Grossman" rel="nofollow noopener">To the End of the Land</a>,</i> though they are painful for rather different reasons. But the very thing that sets this book so impressively apart from its contemporaries is probably also what will make many readers like it less: it is uncompromising in avoiding the spurious tying-up of loose ends. As the book enters its second part and many of the same voices return, we will find our compassion growing and understanding deepening. There will be epiphanies—but they will be small ones. We may never know how everything fits together in every detail, and actually Krauss can be a little cavalier in the connections she does make. But it can be that way in life too, where even in the best of circumstances a perfect reconstruction is unlikely. And for a people who have had the larger part of their heritage erased forever by the Holocaust, it is impossible. Nicole Krauss is their chronicler, chief mourner, and poet.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1189309179568326280" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer10968700437687288792">There are books that are the right ones at the right time. This one was a book at a certain time, maybe not just right, but with rough hewn edges that generally fit, squint the eyes a little, hold a thumb sideways, good enough. Life has thrown me from a moving vehicle and since I wasn't wearing my seat belt, the resulting scrape has left all these exposed nerve endings to be once again scraped by this book.<br/><br/>It wasn't the best read to have on the commute, the jerking of the bus and other people ca</span>
  <span id="freeText10968700437687288792" style="display:none">There are books that are the right ones at the right time. This one was a book at a certain time, maybe not just right, but with rough hewn edges that generally fit, squint the eyes a little, hold a thumb sideways, good enough. Life has thrown me from a moving vehicle and since I wasn't wearing my seat belt, the resulting scrape has left all these exposed nerve endings to be once again scraped by this book.<br /><br />It wasn't the best read to have on the commute, the jerking of the bus and other people causing abrupt breaks in concentration. The jumps from character to character weren't the easiest to track even if there were no interruptions. There is a whole historical and cultural ocean that I just don't have the background to understand. And all the stories ended up having a looser tie than expected.<br /><br />But I recognize the awareness of loneliness in the writer who had neglected all in favor of work. I got swept up in the unrealistic depiction of a woman who could see her lover's unspokens, though it didn't help them. I nearly wept for the father who just couldn't connect with his son, despite the crushing love he had. I was hurt for the husband whose sense of self is upended by the discovery of an old secret. My world is small, my pains personal, my reading close, my life unimportant. That's why these individual stories dug into me, not what I think was an overall thrust of a theme on relating existence and parts=whole something or other. My eyes are on the ground. I hope there are birds overhead, but I don't tend to look up to see them. Besides, based on recent experience, they would just poop on me anyway (damn crows).</span>

<div id="review_565235857" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/565235857" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/565235857?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer565235857" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1681501207421189393">If you are looking for a light and simple story where there's a plot developed in the classic structure, this is not your book.<br/>This is a tough novel, it requires guessing and work on your part, it's like a puzzle that somehow the reader has to put together. And for me, what makes it a great reading, is that you are not conscious of getting close to solving that puzzle, but when you turn the last page everything makes sense in a strange and singular way, like remembering your own memories, throug</span>
  <span id="freeText1681501207421189393" style="display:none">If you are looking for a light and simple story where there's a plot developed in the classic structure, this is not your book.<br />This is a tough novel, it requires guessing and work on your part, it's like a puzzle that somehow the reader has to put together. And for me, what makes it a great reading, is that you are not conscious of getting close to solving that puzzle, but when you turn the last page everything makes sense in a strange and singular way, like remembering your own memories, through flashes and blurred images.<br /><br />Four seemingly unconnected stories in different times and places with only a "desk" in common, as if that desk is the only witness of the lives that cross its path, witness of sorrow, loneliness and loss. And, of course, of love. And as a lot of the characters that appear in the stories are writers or poets, I'd say this book also emanates love for literature and the art of composing in a very natural way.<br />A glimpse of the stories: a fifty year old writer regretting her chosen loneliness remembers a one night stand with a Chilean poet who later is murdered by Pinochet Regime. An old lawyer recently widowed struggles to communicate with one of his sons, with whom he's always felt estranged but whom he loves and hates deeply at the same time. An old writer who takes care of his sick wife discovers secrets from her past he isn't ready to digest. Two atypical brothers with a strange bond struggle trying not to disappoint their distant father. <br /><br />The voices in the stories are poignant and evocative. I found myself rereading twice some paragraphs because of the beauty of some reflexions and a distinct force behind them. The writing style is sublime, the stories flow as in memories, there are no explicit facts or a lineal storyline, it's mostly feelings attached to past times which come like waves, they flow into your system and you finally forget it's a character talking, it could be your own conscience speaking.<br /><br />I also think this is not a book for everybody and that it can become frustrating not knowing where all this rambling is leading, but if you let your mind free of constraint, you'll experience life in its core. Because that's what this book is about: life. And as I have read in some other reviews I wouldn't qualify this novel as oppressing or pessimistic, I'd say it's realistic. Won't we all have to deal with loss and frustration and death some time in our ives? How will our minds process those feelings? You've got the answer in this book. It's your choice to get it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1681501207421189393" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_124493839" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124493839" />
    <a title="Jana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2296903-jana"><img alt="Jana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1387230362p2/2296903.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/124493839?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jana" href="/user/show/2296903-jana">Jana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2296903-jana?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2296903-jana?shelf=heart-and-mind">heart-and-mind</a>
